<div id="review_1870141931" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1870141931" />
    <a title="Sasha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8921530-sasha"><img alt="Sasha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363121783p2/8921530.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1870141931?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sasha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sasha" href="/user/show/8921530-sasha">Sasha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780345443984" href="/book/show/1206503.The_Redemption_of_Althalus">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8921530-sasha?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1870141931" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16222405001020887840">Oh boy. This was a bit of a train wreck. I didn't buy this book and instead swapped it, as I've done so a lot lately, for an old book of mine that was gathering dust. This is one of those that is definitely going to be swapped back. It started a bit slow but somewhat promising, featuring a likable anti-hero, a cat (!!!) and some religious battle that lies in the hands of this anti-hero. However, the 800 pages did little to move things along and the characters upon characters are interchangeable </span>
  <span id="freeText16222405001020887840" style="display:none">Oh boy. This was a bit of a train wreck. I didn't buy this book and instead swapped it, as I've done so a lot lately, for an old book of mine that was gathering dust. This is one of those that is definitely going to be swapped back. It started a bit slow but somewhat promising, featuring a likable anti-hero, a cat (!!!) and some religious battle that lies in the hands of this anti-hero. However, the 800 pages did little to move things along and the characters upon characters are interchangeable at best, annoying at worst. The dialogue is horrendous, repetitive and the exposition is EVERYWHERE. The whole plot is regurgitated every 10 pages or so and don't get me started about the relationships and deus ex machinae that, of course, always favor the heroes. All in all it reads like a bad mash-up of the show Supernatural and Star Trek fan fiction including Vulcan telepathic bonds and Old/Middle English. <br /><br />Let's have a closer look, though. We are in a nondescript country with nondescript people that could or could not live in something that resembles 1000 BC of our time. Our hero Althalus, full time thief and part-time whatever you can hire him for (apparently his HAS murdered, which doesn't concern our hero a bit and does not concern him until the end...which raises a bunch of questions that are never answered). He is lucky in all his criminal endeavors until he goes to the "lowlands" which we can compare him to go down from the Alps to the Mediterranean. His fortune leaves him there so he sees himself forced to accept whatever job he can get - in this case, stealing a book (which, btw, is explained as a leather box with sheets of papers in it. No matter how magical this book is, that is a shitty way to make a book, if it even can be considered such with that weird description). Anyhow, our dear hero travel wide and far to reach "the end of the world" and encounter aforementioned cat. Little does he know that this cat is far more precious than he thinks <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ she happens to be Dweia, God's sister. Not an entirely new concept, but I appreciate the thought. She's very annoying, though  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. She persuades him not to do what he was meant to and instead work for her. After quite some time he finds out that he is destined for more than just thieving around and has to get a group together to fight the forces of evil. If this sounds sorta cliché to you, congratulations, you've just found out what the rest of the 700 pages will be: Pure cliché. Pretty much everything is predictable and takes way too long to get to the point. Once Althalus gets his merry band of helpers together (a barbaric teen warrior, an obnoxious teen empress, a naive priest, a kid thief in the making and an ostracized telepathically empowered witch) they will have to use all the perks that they are offered - which are vast <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ a house that can slow and speed up time for everyone in there, sometimes even separately; windows that can show you any place in the world at any given time; doors that can lead to said places; door frames that manipulate the time frame (no pun intended) you wanna have for those doors. So yeah, you read that right, anytime and anyplace with no big consequences - apparently you just replace yourself in the past. Then they have a whole army of a whole tribe backing them up - the few bad apples easily identified and singled out thanks to the house - with ingenuous tactics that get them ahead of the enemy time and time again. There's also the fact that everyone in the group is perfectly tailored to get them out of bad situations, nothing ever becomes too much. A hit to the head? Easily curable through the house and some telepathy. Flaws in plans? Easily pointed out by a kid who has no business in understanding anything past the shape of the potato he's gonna steal for lunch. Anything needs to magically happen? Let just Althalus use magic words he learned from a magic book or - even more conveniently - just gets them whispered to by Dweia  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. It soon becomes obvious that they're too good, too fast and too intelligent to ever face a real prospect of loss, other than collateral damage. The villains are as stupid as the heroes are cunning. Ghend, Althalus' main adversary, has an equal merry band of misfits that work for him <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ he in turn works for Daeva, the devil, and god's and Dweia's brother <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. However, where Althalus has a group of righteous, beautiful and intelligent followers, Ghend's are ugly, mean and just downright hideous in any way, shape or form. Granted, they are the evil guys, but come on. Did it HAVE to be so literal? Not one of the good guys could have been marred by visible scars? Not one of the evil guys could've use his or her beauty to seduce the masses they were trying to move? As everything works in parallels, it soon becomes obvious that while Ghend has pretty much the same resources as Althalus, he doesn't have the same range <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ with range I mean the flimsy excuse the Dweia gives for this plot device to skew everything in their favor: her explanation of Ghend's limited use of "windows" and "doors" of his equivalent "house" is that her brother has everyone on a tight leash and doesn't trust Ghend enough to let him use them to his advantage. She, as the trustful goddess (which is total BS, she commands and manipulates her way through the whole book), of course, trusts her followers and thus they have access to this and can outsmart Ghend at every damn corner, which makes for a boring, boring book  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <br />All this continues in a very long, very detailed battle you will be loath to see through it's very long duration. I am not kidding when I say it's more than 200 pages worth of gathering an army, finding a location, getting them there, preparing the battlefield, spy on the enemy, change the battlefield, deal with the bad apples in the army and defend the whole empire of the teenage empress - all of which is just pretense to fight Ghend and his goons <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ not very surprisingly the good guys win  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Once you've finally read through the depiction of a bunch of interchangeable barbaric warrior clan chiefs defending this and that, you'll finally be able to move on in the plot. At this point you've still got about 250 pages worth of text and you're wondering why you descended in this maddening pit of repetition. We finally see our guys face of the other guys one by one <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ all of which are disposed of in a fraction of the time the whole damn exposition took place  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. The most aggravating of all the dealings is probably the last, where time travel is used and makes you ask: why not do this at the very beginning? <br />The whole thing ends in somewhat of and open ended setting, where you're neither here nor there but is for all intents and purposes a happy end <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ everyone gets married (the two teens, the teen's mom and his Sergeant, the priest and the witch, that one chief dude with the daughter of the other chief dude...) or a new home and - shocking!! - Dweia and Althalus marry and have a baby. Wow. What a revelation. Such imagination. I know Dweia is supposed to be some kind of goddess of fertility and creation, but damn, that was so in your face that you might as well sprout flowers on your head <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />So far the plot. The world building is actually nice and very fantasy like, I would've loved a map in the front or back of the book, though (I know many fantasy novels don't have them but I'm a sucker for fantastic geography). The description of the places are, for the most part, not too long. The description of events however is another thing. Althalus and other characters recount stories and anecdotes ad infinitum. Same goes for their language; if I never see another Arum say "What is afoot?" it'll be too soon. Many jokes aren't all that funny and reactions fall flat as they seem the same across the board or just repeat themselves so often that you're just fed up with them. <br />This brings me to the language: Not only are the dialogues weird at times, they're very mashed together. When Althalus speaks with telepathy, there is no clear distinction between him and the other person other than a new line, which can be incredibly confusing. Even though the dialogue is written in italics to show that it is a mental conversation, it would help a great deal if it's not every now and then that they specify who's talking. Another problem is that there is no distance: In one instance the kiddy thief and Althalus are having a conversation that involves two other people they had previously been talking to. It is never stated that they moved aside or that the other two left, their conversation is just continued right after the other conversation with no transition and is thus very confusing.<br />The time travel in this novel is wonky at best. Kiddy thief is put there to explain it in his kiddy voice to dumb it down - and even so, more often than not the other characters have to prod him to be more precise - which seems to have been done for the benefit of the reader. After his 15th such "idea" I just wanted to punch kiddy thief in the face. Not only does it sound stupid in his voice, it just doesn't make sense for him to have this ability. If anything it should've been the priest - he's the one with the affinity for space and time. Don't get me wrong, kids can be intelligent, but it just felt incredibly out of character for a 10yo. The time travel is also very inconsistent and is only used in the favor of the good team. Team devil seemingly never or only rarely gets to tamper with the past or the future.<br />This brings me to one of my very big issues with this book: The villains. I've already mentioned that they're as cliché as they come. The few glimpses you get of them had the potential for awesome intrigues and divergent plot lines that complicate everything for the good team, but no, nothing was ever done that was not expected <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ the witch always happens to hear what comes next or the window can be used to spy right next to them and conveniently appear whenever there's something of importance being discussed that is pertinent to the situation of the good guys and as such can be counter acted  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. The only actual PoVs that include the villains are weird dream sections written in Old English that are shared in the group - something I still find stupidly convenient <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ this alerts them to how the future has to pan out and, of course, how to prepare for it and win  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Never do you see anything about their motivations or the entity behind the scenes <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ in fact, you never get to "see" Deava in the whole book  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br />The relationships are also horribly sugary and over the top. There are no surprises <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ for instance, wouldn't it have been awesome with the witch had fancied Althalus and therefore opposed Dweia? Or the empress fallen in love with one of the collateral damage dudes that die, and therefore had tragedy hit? Throw the one or other homosexual character in there? There were ample opportunities to shape interesting bonds and relationships, but the authors took the most obvious ones and went through with them. ALL OF THEM.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <br /><br />So. Predictable, repetitive, slow and with bad dialogues and prose all over the place. Doesn't get more than two stars, even though it had started with four. Ah well. I hope the next person who picks it up might enjoy it somewhat more. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16222405001020887840" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2125787166" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2125787166" />
    <a title="William" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27715535-william"><img alt="William" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1437196271p2/27715535.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2125787166?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="William" class="user" itemprop="url" name="William" href="/user/show/27715535-william">William</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27715535-william?shelf=recommended">recommended</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27715535-william?shelf=bad-bad-bad">bad-bad-bad</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27715535-william?shelf=easy-reads">easy-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27715535-william?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2125787166" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10064212059830711524">It's a little awkward when a book is recommended to you and you find yourself coming out of it with a bit of an eyeroll and more than a little exasperation. <br/><br/>Eddings has some wit and fun with his world, which is more than I can say for many fantasy authors who are so hell-bent on mimicking Tolkien that any sort of levity is a sin. Unfortunately though, that's about where my positives for the book ends. I see a lot of reviews here which decry this work for having familiar Edding's tropes condensed</span>
  <span id="freeText10064212059830711524" style="display:none">It's a little awkward when a book is recommended to you and you find yourself coming out of it with a bit of an eyeroll and more than a little exasperation. <br /><br />Eddings has some wit and fun with his world, which is more than I can say for many fantasy authors who are so hell-bent on mimicking Tolkien that any sort of levity is a sin. Unfortunately though, that's about where my positives for the book ends. I see a lot of reviews here which decry this work for having familiar Edding's tropes condensed into a predictable mess, and without even reading another of his works I can say that is probably the case. The villains are bland and honestly portrayed as having just about two brain cells between them, making them all (with the exception of one undead dude) completely non-threatening to our heroes. There is never a sense of urgency in a plot which allows its heroes to time and space hop, deliver armies whenever needed, retrieve endless funds, etc. The attempts Eddings does have to create tension only barely succeed - Eliar being clubbed on the back of the head, for example, limits the groups space-hopping abilities for a bit, but the heroes' army is never put in any real state of duress. They continue to pretty easily hold off the idiotic assaults of the enemy, retreating to a conveniently placed mountain for no reason other than to really beat it into the villain's heads that they are absolutely as stupid as they act. In another scene, our heroes burn the crops ahead of the invading army in order to starve them out, and then have to deal with the consequences of that food shortage for the land they are trying to protect... until surprise! The neighboring country actually had a great harvest, and we'll just use some of that endless money to fill the graineries and ta-dah! No problems! The entire book is a sad hodge-podge of such instances back to back, making for slow pacing and episodic predictability. Something always goes a bit wrong, only to be corrected with no consequence because... plot? Oh, the enemies got a giant army in your city and you guys can't fend them off? Stab one guy and they all turn out to be an illusion that just poofs! Yikes, dude. <br /><br />Beyond this, the characters were often left lackluster and confusing in their motivations. The seven main characters are fairly reducible to one trait (Eliar likes food &amp; soldiering, Gher is an original thinker and resident expositionist, Bheid has a sense of righteous justice and cannot for the life of him accept reality, etc.) which works passably with some like Andine, but makes the others fall horribly flat. Entire character arcs are introduced and then never resolved for growth. Althalus, our titular hero, doesn't really develop - he's still the same sneaky, okay-with-murdering, money-loving "rogue" from the beginning, only now he likes a cat-goddess. Okay. That's not really worthy of a title like <i>The Redemption of Althalus</i>, but surely that was just a misstep, right? A really big, glaring, giant-font mistake... oh dear. But the most egregious example being Bheid's despair after taking his first kill, in which he essentially mopes for a bit and is just told to get over it and does - showing no shift in perspective or lessons learned. They even set up a scene where he is going to officiate a wedding, where I thought perhaps Eddings would give him a moment in his speech where he addressed death and life and his newfound perspective in order to let us understand it - but they just cut right past the wedding altogether. <br /><br />And the weddings... oh, the weddings. Everyone had to pair up, which is one of my least favorite things a novelist can do. Some, like Eliar and Andine, were admittedly alright; they're teenagers, so their romance made sense, but Bheid and Leitha are so tired as standalone characters that their pointless hook up was eyeroll-inducing. I don't know about his other works, but Eddings comes off here as profoundly misunderstanding romance, and women, really - I was especially turned off with his tendency to make women smugly doing figure 8's around their idiotic men, as if we as an audience are supposed to laugh along and say, "Oh, <i>men</i>!" Meanwhile the only female villain is singularly and repeatedly characterized only by how ugly she is - a line of insult that is not ever pointed toward our male antagonists. Gelta is ugly beyond all reason, because that's what makes women bad, I guess; they're all pimply and bulbous and stinky and just ugly ugly ugly, huh? And let's not talk about how often she is referred to as a bitch, cow, whore, and another host of expletives never raised to her male counterparts. Way to break down barriers, Eddings. <br /><br />The amount of exposition in this book actually killed me a little inside. Eddings needs like, three more editors to go through and tell him to cut stuff out. Remember that wedding scene with Bheid I mentioned earlier that could have been good for resolving an emotional arc but was instead skipped? Well, Eddings definitely felt that was too much to write about, and yet doesn't waste a second to tirelessly repeat verbatim exposition that we as readers have already been made aware of to other characters. This guy is the king of useless exposition; I cannot count the amount of times the characters lengthy explanations of the in-universe magic or their next move were completely hashed out to us as readers, only to be told to us <i>again</i> whenever a new character arrives and says "wait, so what are you guys doing?" It was quite literally exhausting. Gher explains the concepts of their space and time-hopping not once, not twice, but on <i>eight separate occasions</i>, each time being as lengthy and repetitive as the first. Eddings is also the worst offender of the "show, don't tell" rule that I've read in a long time; we are told Althalus is an awesome thief all the time, although we never see it; we are told Leitha is a jokester, although her "jokes" consist of two occasions where she makes Bheid a little uncomfortable and teases Althalus by awkwardly calling him "daddy" (a thing that is already cringy but even more so when put against her rape-backstory as context). We're told the evil god wants to rule the world or something, but we never quite get an idea of what it is he wants to do with it. There is so little ever shown that by the end of the book I was still left unsure as to exactly what the god's books were - did they house the respective god's power or essence? Would destroying them destroy the god? Did they each contain different magical powers? Who knows. <br /><br />In short, everything about this was hopelessly contrived. This is the type of book where, if it weren't a laborous 800 pages, I would give to a creative writing class to read to learn how NOT to write a story. It really fails on that many fronts. What little possible potential the set-up for this story had was squandered by horrific pacing, endless repetitiveness, bad characterization, cringy depictions of women that bordered on misogynistic, fat-shaming, and a strange penchant for setting up religious straw-man through which Eddings seemed to angrily scream at the audience that religion was stupid and limited and anyone who believed in anything other than what was right in front of them is obviously being conned. I gotta rate this 1/5, would really not ever recommend, and feel super bad since a friend offered it to me as an example of the type of story he liked. We're... gonna have to have some words. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10064212059830711524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('MB4MmeWE4pRHtEPKqgEpMxSqJ6mbyfwbhV8pb0kD5cZAf3MuvZxR5wIvxXyJUGTX4DL+WCYQFFI3LYP9nCgT2A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

Feist introduces a new generation of readers to his riveting novel of adventure and intrigue, revised and updated as he always meant it to be written. It is a work that explores strength and weakness, hope and fear, and what it means to be a man—in a kingdom where peace is the most precious commodity of all.If there were two more impetuous and carefree men in the Kingdom of the Isles, they had yet to be found. Twins Borric and Erland wore that mantle proudly, much to the chagrin of their father, Prince Arutha of Krondor. But their blissful youth has come to an end. Their uncle, the King, has produced no male children. Bypassing himself, Arutha names Borric, the eldest twin by seconds, the Royal Heir. As his brother, Erland will have his own great responsibilities to shoulder. To drive home their future roles, Arutha sends them as ambassadors to Kesh, the most feared nation in the world. Borric and Erland will be presented to the Queen of Kesh—the single most powerful ruler in the known world—at her Seventy-fifth Jubilee Anniversary. But they have not even left Krondor when an assassination attempt on Borric is thwarted. Aware that he is being provoked into war, Arutha does not rise to the bait. His sons’ journey will not be deterred, for nothing less than peace is riding on it. Yet there is to be no peace for the young princes. When their traveling party is ambushed, Borric disappears and is presumed dead—sending Erland into spirals of rage and grief as he is forced to navigate alone the court intrigues at Kesh. But unbeknownst to anyone, Borric lives and has escaped his captors. In a strange land, with a price on his head, Borric must use all his wits and stamina to find his way back to his brother. On separate paths, the two men—one a fugitive and one a future king—make their journey toward maturity, honor, and duty.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/8732.David_Eddings">About David Eddings</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/8732.David_Eddings"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1223870462p3/8732.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer532049933284487278">David Eddings was an American author who wrote several best-selling series of epic fantasy novels. David Eddings' wife, <b>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8732.Leigh_Eddings' title='Leigh Eddings' rel='nofollow noopener'>Leigh Eddings</a>
</b>, was an uncredited co-author on many of his early books, but he had later acknowledged that she contributed to them all.<br/><br/>David Eddings' first books (which were general fiction) sold moderately well. He later switched to writing epic fantasy, a field in which he a</span>
  <span id="freeText532049933284487278" style="display:none">David Eddings was an American author who wrote several best-selling series of epic fantasy novels. David Eddings' wife, <b>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8732.Leigh_Eddings" title="Leigh Eddings" rel="nofollow noopener">Leigh Eddings</a>
</b>, was an uncredited co-author on many of his early books, but he had later acknowledged that she contributed to them all.<br /><br />David Eddings' first books (which were general fiction) sold moderately well. He later switched to writing epic fantasy, a field in which he achieved great success. In a recent interview with sffworld.com, he said: "I don't take orders from readers."<br /><br />On January 26, 2007 it was reported that Eddings accidentally burned about a quarter of his office, next door to his house, along with his Excalibur sports car, and the original manuscripts for most of his novels. He was flushing the fuel tank of the car with water when he lit a piece of paper and threw into the puddle to test if it was still flammable.<br /><br />On February 28, 2007, David Eddings' wife, Leigh Eddings (born Judith Leigh Schall), died following a series of strokes. She was 69.<br /><br />David Eddings died on June 2, 2009 at the age of 77.</span>
  <a data-text-id="532049933284487278" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
